class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" American Anglican Conference</a></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><span style="word-spacing:0">This box: </span><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Navbar" title="Template:Navbar"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Navbar" title="Template talk:Navbar"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Navbar&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>\n<a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Canterbury cathedral.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Canterbury_cathedral.jpg/16px-Canterbury_cathedral.jpg" width="16" height="12" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Canterbury_cathedral.jpg/24px-Canterbury_cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Canterbury_cathedral.jpg/32px-Canterbury_cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Anglicanism" title="Portal:Anglicanism">Anglicanism&#32;portal</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>English Reformation</b> was a series of events in 16th-century England by which the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> broke away from the authority of the Pope and the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>. These events were, in part, associated with the wider process of the European <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, a religious and political movement that affected the practice of Christianity across western and central Europe during this period. Many factors contributed to the process: the decline of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> and the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>, the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> and increased circulation of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, and the transmission of new knowledge and ideas among scholars, the upper and middle classes and readers in general. However, the various phases of the English Reformation, which also covered <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, were largely driven by changes in government policy, to which public opinion gradually accommodated itself.\n</p><p>Based on <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>\'s desire for an annulment of his marriage (first requested of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VII" title="Pope Clement VII">Pope Clement VII</a> in 1527), the English Reformation was at the outset more of a political affair than a theological dispute. The reality of political differences between Rome and England allowed growing theological disputes to come to the fore.<sup id="cite_ref-scruton1996p470_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scruton1996p470-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Until the break with Rome, it was the Pope and general councils of the Church that decided <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a>. Church law was governed by <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> with final jurisdiction in Rome. Church taxes were paid straight to Rome, and the Pope had the final word in the appointment of bishops.\n</p><p>The break with Rome was effected by a series of acts of Parliament passed between 1532 and 1534, among them the 1534 <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Supremacy" title="Acts of Supremacy">Act of Supremacy</a>, which declared that Henry was the "Supreme Head on earth of the Church of England".<sup id="cite_ref-bray_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bray-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> (This title was renounced by <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a> in 1553 in the process of restoring papal jurisdiction; when <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> reasserted the royal supremacy in 1559, her title was <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Governor">Supreme Governor</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-bray_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bray-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Final authority in doctrinal and legal disputes now rested with the monarch, and the papacy was deprived of revenue and the final say on the appointment of bishops.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_liturgy" title="Christian liturgy">liturgy</a> of the Church of England became markedly <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> during the reign of Henry\'s son <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a> largely along lines laid down by Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>. Under Mary, the whole process was reversed and the Church of England was again placed under papal jurisdiction. Soon after, Elizabeth reintroduced the Protestant faith but in a more moderate manner. The structure and theology of the church was a matter of fierce dispute for generations.\n</p><p>The violent aspect of these disputes, manifested in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil Wars</a>, ended when the last Roman Catholic monarch, <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II</a>, was deposed, and Parliament asked <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William III</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_II_of_England" title="Mary II of England">Mary II</a> to rule jointly in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/English_Bill_of_Rights" class="mw-redirect" title="English Bill of Rights">English Bill of Rights</a> in 1688 (in the "<a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>"), from which emerged a church polity with an <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">established church</a> and a number of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">non-conformist</a> churches whose members at first suffered various <a href="/wiki/Civil_disabilities" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil disabilities">civil disabilities</a> that were removed over time. The legacy of the past Roman Catholic Establishment remained an issue for some time, and still exists today. A substantial minority remained Roman Catholic in England, and in an effort to disestablish it from British systems, their church organisation remained illegal <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_England_and_Wales#Nineteenth_century" title="Catholic Church in England and Wales">until the 19th century</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" 1558</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Act_of_Uniformity_1558"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Act of Uniformity 1558</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Puritans_and_Roman_Catholics"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Puritans and Roman Catholics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Historiography_2"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_VIII:_marriages_and_desire_for_a_male_heir">Henry VIII: marriages and desire for a male heir</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Henry VIII: marriages and desire for a male heir">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein,_the_Younger,_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/150px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="150" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/225px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/300px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger%2C_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2994" data-file-height="4203" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein,_the_Younger,_Around_1497-1543_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_of_England_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Holbein_the_Younger" title="Hans Holbein the Younger">Hans Holbein the Younger</a>. <a href="/wiki/Thyssen-Bornemisza_Museum" title="Thyssen-Bornemisza Museum">Thyssen-Bornemisza Museum</a>, Madrid.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Catherine_aragon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Catherine_aragon.jpg/220px-Catherine_aragon.jpg" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Catherine_aragon.jpg/330px-Catherine_aragon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Catherine_aragon.jpg/440px-Catherine_aragon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="616" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Catherine_aragon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, Henry VIII\'s first wife. Attributed to <a href="/wiki/Joannes_Corvus" title="Joannes Corvus">Joannes Corvus</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery,_London" title="National Portrait Gallery, London">National Portrait Gallery, London</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> acceded to the English throne in 1509 at the age of 17. He made a dynastic marriage with <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, widow of his brother <a href="/wiki/Arthur,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Arthur, Prince of Wales">Arthur</a>, in June 1509, just before his coronation on <a href="/wiki/Midsummer" title="Midsummer">Midsummer\'s Day</a>. Unlike his <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">father</a>, who was secretive and conservative, the young Henry appeared the epitome of chivalry and sociability. An observant <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, he heard up to five <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">masses</a> a day (except during the hunting season); of "powerful but unoriginal mind", he let himself be influenced by his advisors from whom he was never apart, by night or day. He was thus susceptible to whoever had his ear.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This contributed to a state of hostility between his young contemporaries and the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolsey" title="Thomas Wolsey">Thomas Wolsey</a>. As long as Wolsey had his ear, Henry\'s Roman Catholicism was secure: in 1521, he had defended the Roman Catholic Church from <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>\'s accusations of <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> in a book he wrote\xe2\x80\x94probably with considerable help from the conservative <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Rochester" title="Bishop of Rochester">Bishop of Rochester</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher" title="John Fisher">John Fisher</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94entitled <i>The <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Seven_Sacraments" title="Defence of the Seven Sacraments">Defence of the Seven Sacraments</a></i>, for which he was awarded the title "Defender of the Faith" (<i><a href="/wiki/Fidei_Defensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Fidei Defensor">Fidei Defensor</a></i>) by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Pope Leo X</a>. (Successive English and British monarchs have retained this title to the present, even after the Anglican Church broke away from Roman Catholicism, in part because the title was re-conferred by Parliament in 1544, after the split.) Wolsey\'s enemies at court included those who had been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> ideas,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000111_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000111-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> among whom was the attractive, charismatic <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>.\n</p><p>Anne arrived at court in 1522 as <a href="/wiki/Maid_of_honour" title="Maid of honour">maid of honour</a> to Queen Catherine, having spent some years in France being educated by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Claude" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Claude">Queen Claude</a> of France. She was a woman of "charm, style and wit, with will and savagery which made her a match for Henry."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Anne was a distinguished French conversationalist, singer, and dancer. She was cultured and is the disputed author of several songs and poems.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>  By 1527, Henry wanted his marriage to Catherine <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_nullity" title="Declaration of nullity">annulled</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> She had not produced a male heir who survived longer than two months, and Henry wanted a son to secure the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a>. Before Henry\'s father (<a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>) ascended the throne, England had been beset by <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">civil warfare</a> over rival claims to the English crown. Henry wanted to avoid a similar uncertainty over the succession.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Catherine of Aragon\'s only surviving child was <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Princess Mary</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_boleyn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Anne_boleyn.jpg/220px-Anne_boleyn.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Anne_boleyn.jpg/330px-Anne_boleyn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Anne_boleyn.jpg/440px-Anne_boleyn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2401" data-file-height="3150" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anne_boleyn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>, Henry VIII\'s second wife, by an unknown artist. National Portrait Gallery, London.</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry claimed that this lack of a male heir was because his marriage was "blighted in the eyes of God".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Catherine had been his <a href="/wiki/Arthur,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Arthur, Prince of Wales">late brother</a>\'s wife, and it was therefore <a href="/wiki/Affinity_(canon_law)#Mosaic_law" title="Affinity (canon law)">against biblical teachings</a> for Henry to have married her (<a href="/wiki/Leviticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Leviticus">Leviticus</a> 20:21); a special <a href="/wiki/Dispensation_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispensation (Catholic Church)">dispensation</a> from <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a> had been needed to allow the wedding in the first place.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Henry argued the marriage was never valid because the biblical prohibition was part of unbreakable divine law, and even popes could not dispense with it.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1527, Henry asked <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VII" title="Pope Clement VII">Pope Clement VII</a> to annul the marriage, but the Pope refused. According to <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">canon law</a>, the pope could not annul a marriage on the basis of a <a href="/wiki/Canonical_impediment" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical impediment">canonical impediment</a> previously dispensed. Clement also feared the wrath of Catherine\'s nephew, <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a>, whose troops earlier that year had <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(1527)" title="Sack of Rome (1527)">sacked Rome</a> and briefly taken the Pope prisoner.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The combination of his "scruple of conscience" and his captivation by Anne Boleyn made his desire to rid himself of his Queen compelling.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000114_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000114-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The indictment of his chancellor Cardinal Wolsey in 1529 for <a href="/wiki/Praemunire" title="Praemunire">praemunire</a> (taking the authority of the papacy above the Crown), and subsequent death in November 1530 on his way to London to answer a charge of high treason left Henry open to the opposing influences of the supporters of the Queen and those who sanctioned the abandonment of the Roman allegiance, for whom an annulment was but an opportunity.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_debate_and_legislation">Parliamentary debate and legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Parliamentary debate and legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1529, the King summoned <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a> to deal with annulment, thus bringing together those who wanted reform but who disagreed what form it should take; it became known as the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation_Parliament" title="English Reformation Parliament">Reformation Parliament</a>. There were common lawyers who resented the privileges of the clergy to summon <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">laity</a> to their courts;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh73_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh73-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> there were those who had been influenced by Lutheranism and were hostile to the theology of Rome; <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell" title="Thomas Cromwell">Thomas Cromwell</a> was both. Henry\'s chancellor, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>, successor to Wolsey, also wanted reform: he wanted new laws against heresy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000116_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000116-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell,Thomas(1EEssex)01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/220px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg" width="220" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/330px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg/440px-Cromwell%2CThomas%281EEssex%2901.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2768" data-file-height="3312" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell,Thomas(1EEssex)01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex">Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex</a> (c. 1485\xe2\x80\x931540), Henry VIII\'s chief minister 1532\xe2\x80\x9340.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cromwell was a lawyer and a member of Parliament\xe2\x80\x94a Protestant who saw how Parliament could be used to advance the Royal Supremacy, which Henry wanted, and to further Protestant beliefs and practices Cromwell and his friends wanted.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> One of his closest friends was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>, soon to be made an <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">archbishop</a>.\n</p><p>In the matter of the annulment, no progress seemed possible. The Pope seemed more afraid of Emperor Charles V than of Henry. Anne and Cromwell and their allies wished simply to ignore the Pope, but in October 1530 a meeting of clergy and lawyers advised that Parliament could not empower the archbishop to act against the Pope\'s prohibition. Henry thus resolved to bully the <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993105\xe2\x80\x93106_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993105\xe2\x80\x93106-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Actions_by_Henry_against_English_clergy">Actions by Henry against English clergy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Actions by Henry against English clergy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Having brought down his chancellor, Cardinal Wolsey, Henry VIII finally resolved to charge the whole English clergy with praemunire to secure their agreement to his annulment. The <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Praemunire" title="Statute of Praemunire">Statute of Praemunire</a>, which forbade obedience to the authority of the Pope or of any foreign rulers, enacted in 1392, had been used against individuals in the ordinary course of court proceedings. Now Henry, having first charged Queen Catherine\'s supporters, Bishops <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher" title="John Fisher">John Fisher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_West" title="Nicholas West">Nicholas West</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Standish" title="Henry Standish">Henry Standish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archdeacon_of_Exeter" title="Archdeacon of Exeter">Archdeacon of Exeter</a>, Adam Travers, decided to proceed against the whole clergy.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Henry claimed \xc2\xa3100,000 from the <a href="/wiki/Convocation_of_the_English_Clergy" class="mw-redirect" title="Convocation of the English Clergy">Convocation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Canterbury" title="Province of Canterbury">Canterbury</a> (a representative body of English clergy) for their pardon, which was granted by the Convocation on 24 January 1531. The clergy wanted the payment spread over five years, but Henry refused. The convocation responded by withdrawing their payment altogether and demanded Henry fulfill certain guarantees before they would give him the money. Henry refused these conditions. He agreed only to the five-year period of payment and added five articles that specified that:\n</p>\n<ol><li>The clergy recognise Henry as the "sole protector and Supreme Head of the Church and clergy of England"<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The King had spiritual <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a></li>\n<li>The privileges of the Church were upheld only if they did not detract from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a> and the laws of the realm</li>\n<li>The King pardoned the clergy for violating the statute of praemunire, and</li>\n<li>The laity were also pardoned.</li></ol>\n<p>In Parliament, Bishop Fisher championed Catherine and the clergy; he had inserted into the first article the phrase "...as far as the word of God allows..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000118_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000118-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanner1930_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETanner1930-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In Convocation, however, <a href="/wiki/William_Warham" title="William Warham">William Warham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, requested a discussion but was met by a stunned silence; then Warham said, "He who is silent seems to consent," to which a clergyman responded, "Then we are all silent." The Convocation granted consent to the King\'s five articles and the payment on 8 March 1531. That same year, Parliament passed the Pardon to Clergy Act 1531.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_legislative_acts">Further legislative acts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Further legislative acts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The breaking of the power of Rome proceeded little by little. In 1532, Cromwell brought before Parliament the <i><a href="/wiki/Supplication_Against_the_Ordinaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Supplication Against the Ordinaries">Supplication Against the Ordinaries</a></i>, which listed nine grievances against the Church, including abuses of power and Convocation\'s independent legislative power. Finally, on 10 May, the King demanded of Convocation that the Church renounce all authority to make laws. On 15 May, the <a href="/wiki/Submission_of_the_Clergy" title="Submission of the Clergy">Submission of the Clergy</a> was subscribed, which recognised <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Supremacy" title="Acts of Supremacy">Royal Supremacy</a> over the Church so that it could no longer make canon law without royal licence\xe2\x80\x94i.e., without the King\'s permission\xe2\x80\x94thus emasculating it as a law-making body. (Parliament subsequently passed this in 1534 and again in 1536.) The day after this, More resigned as chancellor, leaving Cromwell as Henry\'s chief minister. (Cromwell never became chancellor. His power came\xe2\x80\x94and was lost\xe2\x80\x94through his informal relations with Henry.)\n</p><p>Several acts of Parliament then followed. The Act in Conditional Restraint of Annates proposed that the clergy pay no more than 5 percent of their first year\'s revenue (<a href="/wiki/Annates" title="Annates">annates</a>) to Rome. This was initially controversial and required that Henry visit the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> three times to browbeat the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">Commons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein,_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Hans_Holbein%2C_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4667" data-file-height="5807" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hans_Holbein,_the_Younger_-_Sir_Thomas_More_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>, with <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher" title="John Fisher">John Fisher</a> the leader of political resistance against the break with Rome. Both were executed in 1535.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Statute_in_Restraint_of_Appeals" title="Statute in Restraint of Appeals">Act in Restraint of Appeals</a>, drafted by Cromwell, apart from outlawing appeals to Rome on ecclesiastical matters, declared that\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This realm of England is an Empire, and so hath been accepted in the world, governed by one Supreme Head and King having the dignity and royal estate of the Imperial Crown of the same, unto whom a body politic compact of all sorts and degrees of people divided in terms and by names of Spirituality and Temporality, be bounden and owe to bear next to God a natural and humble obedience.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>This declared England an independent country in every respect. English historian <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Elton" title="Geoffrey Elton">Geoffrey Elton</a> called this act an "essential ingredient" of the "Tudor revolution" in that it expounded a theory of <a href="/wiki/National_sovereignty" class="mw-redirect" title="National sovereignty">national sovereignty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Act_in_Absolute_Restraint_of_Annates" class="mw-redirect" title="Act in Absolute Restraint of Annates">Act in Absolute Restraint of Annates</a> outlawed all annates to Rome and also ordered that if <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedrals</a> refused the King\'s nomination for bishop, they would be liable to punishment by praemunire. Finally in 1534, the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Supremacy" title="Acts of Supremacy">Acts of Supremacy</a> made Henry "supreme head in earth of the Church of England" and disregarded any "usage, custom, foreign laws, foreign authority [or] prescription".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElton1982364\xe2\x80\x93365_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElton1982364\xe2\x80\x93365-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, having taken Anne to France on a pre-nuptial honeymoon, Henry married her in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> in January 1533. This was made easier by the death of Archbishop Warham, a strong opponent of an annulment. Henry appointed Thomas Cranmer to succeed him as Archbishop of Canterbury. Cranmer was prepared to grant the annulment of the marriage to Catherine as Henry required, going so far as to pronounce the judgment that Henry\'s marriage with Catherine was against the law of God on 23 May.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Anne gave birth to a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Princess Elizabeth</a>, in September 1533. The Pope responded to the marriage by <a href="/wiki/Excommunication#The_Catholic_Church" title="Excommunication">excommunicating</a> both Henry and Cranmer from the Roman Catholic Church (11 July 1533).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Henry was excommunicated again in December 1538.\n</p><p>Consequently, in the same year the Act of First Fruits and Tenths transferred the taxes on ecclesiastical income from the Pope to the Crown. The <a href="/wiki/Act_Concerning_Peter%27s_Pence_and_Dispensations" title="Act Concerning Peter&#39;s Pence and Dispensations">Act Concerning Peter\'s Pence and Dispensations</a> outlawed the annual payment by landowners of <a href="/wiki/Peter%27s_Pence" title="Peter&#39;s Pence">one penny</a> to the Pope. This Act also reiterated that England had "no superior under God, but only your Grace" and that Henry\'s "imperial crown" had been diminished by "the unreasonable and uncharitable usurpations and exactions" of the Pope.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In case any of this should be resisted, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Treasons_Act_1534" title="Treasons Act 1534">Treasons Act 1534</a>, which made it high treason punishable by death to deny Royal Supremacy. The following year, Thomas More and John Fisher were executed under this legislation. Finally, in 1536, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_against_the_Pope%27s_Authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Act against the Pope&#39;s Authority">Act against the Pope\'s Authority</a>, which removed the last part of papal authority still legal. This was Rome\'s power in England to decide disputes concerning <a href="/wiki/Scripture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scripture">Scripture</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reform_movements">Early reform movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early reform movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:auto;"><caption style="padding-bottom:0.2em;font-size:125%;line-height:1.2em;font-weight:bold">History of Christianity<br />in England</caption><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nGeneral</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_England_and_Wales" title="Catholic Church in England and Wales">Catholic Church in England and Wales</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">Calendar of saints<br />(Church of England)</a><br /></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nEarly</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<a href="/wiki/Joseph_of_Arimathea" title="Joseph of Arimathea">Joseph of Arimathea</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Glastonbury#History_and_mythology" title="Glastonbury">Legend of Christ in Britain</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain#Christianity" title="Roman Britain">Christianity in Roman Britain</a></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nPost-Roman</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Christianity" title="Anglo-Saxon Christianity">Anglo-Saxon Christianity</a><br /></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nReformation</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a><br /><a class="mw-selflink selflink">English Reformation</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Marian_persecutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Marian persecutions">Marian persecutions</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Oxford_Martyrs" title="Oxford Martyrs">Oxford Martyrs</a></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">\nPost-Reformation</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Church_of_England#Puritanism_and_the_Restoration" title="History of the Church of England">Puritanism and the Restoration</a><br /><a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a><br /><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Church_of_England#18th_century" title="History of the Church of England">18th Century Church of England</a><br /><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Church_of_England#19th_century" title="History of the Church of England">19th Century Church of England</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a><br /><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Church_of_England#Recent_history" title="History of the Church of England">Church of England (Recent)</a><br /></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Christianity_in_England" title="Template:History of Christianity in England"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Christianity_in_England" title="Template talk:History of Christianity in England"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Christianity_in_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The break with Rome was not, by itself, a Reformation.<sup id="cite_ref-scruton1996p470_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scruton1996p470-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> However, the political break with Rome was inseparable from the success of Protestantism. English Catholicism was strong and popular in the early 1500s, and while there were those who held Protestant sympathies, they would have remained a religious minority if political events had not intervened.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh199320,28_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh199320,28-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An earlier reform movement that anticipated some Protestant teachings but remained outside the religious mainstream was <a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a>. Derived from the writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, a 14th-century theologian and Bible translator, Lollardy stressed the <a href="/wiki/Prima_scriptura" title="Prima scriptura">primacy of Scripture</a> and emphasised the <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">preaching of the word</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">sacrament of the altar</a>, holding the latter to be but a <a href="/wiki/Memorialism" title="Memorialism">memorial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden200086f_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden200086f-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Unlike Protestants, the early Lollards lacked access to the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> and failed to gain a foothold among the church\'s most popular communicators, the <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friars</a>. Unable to gain access to the levers of power, the Lollards were much reduced in numbers and influence by the 15th century. They sometimes faced investigation and persecution and rarely produced new literature after 1450.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch200336_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch200336-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Lollards could still be found\xe2\x80\x94especially in London and the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley" title="Thames Valley">Thames Valley</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> and even in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">North</a>\xe2\x80\x94and many would be receptive to Protestant ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>More respectable and orthodox calls for reform came from <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanists" class="mw-redirect" title="Renaissance humanists">Renaissance humanists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> (who lived in England for a time), <a href="/wiki/John_Colet" title="John Colet">John Colet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dean_of_St_Paul%27s" title="Dean of St Paul&#39;s">Dean of St Paul\'s</a>, and Thomas More. Humanists downplayed the role of rites and ceremonies in achieving salvation and criticised the superstitious veneration of <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a>. Erasmus and Colet emphasised a simple, personal piety and a return <i><a href="/wiki/Ad_fontes" title="Ad fontes">ad fontes</a></i>, back to the sources of Christian faith\xe2\x80\x94the scriptures as understood through textual and linguistic scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall201729\xe2\x80\x9332_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall201729\xe2\x80\x9332-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Colet\'s commentaries on the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Pauline epistles</a> emphasized <a href="/wiki/Double_predestination" class="mw-redirect" title="Double predestination">double predestination</a> and the worthlessness of human works. Anne Boleyn\'s own religious views were shaped by French humanists such as <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Lef%C3%A8vre_d%27%C3%89taples" title="Jacques Lef\xc3\xa8vre d&#39;\xc3\x89taples">Jacques Lef\xc3\xa8vre d\'\xc3\x89taples</a>, whose 1512 commentaries on Paul\'s epistles stated that human works were irrelevant to <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">salvation</a> five years before Luther published the same views.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003112\xe2\x80\x93111_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003112\xe2\x80\x93111-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017164_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017164-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Humanist scholarship provided arguments against <a href="/wiki/Papal_primacy" title="Papal primacy">papal primacy</a> and support for the claim that popes had usurped powers that rightfully belonged to kings. In 1534, <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">Lorenzo Valla</a>\'s <i>On the <a href="/wiki/Donation_of_Constantine" title="Donation of Constantine">Donation of Constantine</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94which proved that one of the pillars of the papacy\'s temporal authority was a hoax\xe2\x80\x94was published in London. Cromwell paid for an English translation of <a href="/wiki/Marsiglio_of_Padua" class="mw-redirect" title="Marsiglio of Padua">Marsiglio of Padua</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Defensor_pacis" title="Defensor pacis">Defensor pacis</a></i> in 1535. The conservative <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gardiner" title="Stephen Gardiner">Stephen Gardiner</a> used Marsiglio\'s theory of a unitary realm to defend the royal supremacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993123_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993123-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the early 1520s, the views of German reformer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> were known and disputed in England.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The main plank of <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_Martin_Luther" title="Theology of Martin Luther">Luther\'s theology</a> was <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">justification by faith alone</a> rather than by <a href="/wiki/Merit_(Christianity)" title="Merit (Christianity)">good works</a>. In this view, only <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity" title="Faith in Christianity">faith</a>, itself a gift from God, can secure the <a href="/wiki/Grace_in_Christianity" title="Grace in Christianity">grace</a> of God. Justification by faith alone threatened the whole basis of the Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Sacrament_of_Penance" title="Sacrament of Penance">penitential system</a> with its doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prayer_for_the_dead" title="Prayer for the dead">prayer for the dead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indulgence" title="Indulgence">indulgences</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrificial</a> character of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Mass in the Catholic Church">mass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003119\xe2\x80\x93122,130_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003119\xe2\x80\x93122,130-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017126_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017126-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Early Protestants portrayed Catholic practices such as confession to priests, <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy_(Catholic_Church)" title="Clerical celibacy (Catholic Church)">clerical celibacy</a>, and requirements to <a href="/wiki/Fasting_and_abstinence_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Fasting and abstinence in the Catholic Church">fast</a> and keep vows as burdensome and spiritually oppressive. Not only did purgatory lack any biblical basis according to Protestants, but the clergy were accused of using fear of purgatory to make money from prayers and masses. Catholics countered that justification by faith alone was a "licence to sin".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017146_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017146-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  \n</p><p>Protestant ideas were popular among some parts of the English population, especially among academics and merchants with connections to continental Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003202\xe2\x80\x93203_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003202\xe2\x80\x93203-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The first open demonstration of support for Luther took place at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> in 1521 when a student defaced a copy of the <a href="/wiki/Exsurge_Domine" title="Exsurge Domine">papal bull of condemnation</a> against Luther.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017124_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017124-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Also at Cambridge was a group of reform-minded university students that met at the White Horse tavern from the mid-1520s, known by the moniker "Little Germany". Its members included <a href="/wiki/Robert_Barnes_(martyr)" title="Robert Barnes (martyr)">Robert Barnes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Latimer" title="Hugh Latimer">Hugh Latimer</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Frith" title="John Frith">John Frith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bilney" title="Thomas Bilney">Thomas Bilney</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Joye" title="George Joye">George Joye</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arthur_(dramatist)" title="Thomas Arthur (dramatist)">Thomas Arthur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh199358_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh199358-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The publication of <a href="/wiki/William_Tyndale" title="William Tyndale">William Tyndale</a>\'s English New Testament in 1526 helped to spread Protestant ideas. Printed abroad and smuggled into the country, the <a href="/wiki/Tyndale_Bible" title="Tyndale Bible">Tyndale Bible</a> was the first English Bible to be mass produced; there were probably 16,000 copies in England by 1536. Tyndale\'s translation was highly influential, forming the basis of all later English translations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003203_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003203-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> An attack on traditional religion, Tyndale\'s translation included an epilogue explaining Luther\'s theology of justification by faith, and many translation choices were designed to undermine traditional Catholic teachings. Tyndale translated the Greek word <i>charis</i> as <i>favour</i> rather than <i>grace</i> to de-emphasize the role of grace-giving sacraments. His choice of <i>love</i> rather than <i>charity</i> to translate <i><a href="/wiki/Agape" title="Agape">agape</a></i> de-emphasized good works. When rendering the Greek verb <i><a href="/wiki/Metanoia_(theology)" title="Metanoia (theology)">metanoeite</a></i> into English, Tyndale used <i><a href="/wiki/Repentance_(Christianity)" title="Repentance (Christianity)">repent</a></i> rather than <i>do penance</i>. The former word indicated an internal turning to God, while the latter translation supported the sacrament of confession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017132_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017132-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>As chancellor, Thomas More pursued an aggressive campaign against heresy. Between 1530 and 1533, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hitton" title="Thomas Hitton">Thomas Hitton</a> (England\'s first Protestant martyr), Thomas Bilney, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bayfield" title="Richard Bayfield">Richard Bayfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Tewkesbury" title="John Tewkesbury">John Tewkesbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Bainham" title="James Bainham">James Bainham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Benet_(martyr)" title="Thomas Benet (martyr)">Thomas Benet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harding" title="Thomas Harding">Thomas Harding</a>, John Frith and Andrew Hewet were burned to death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017186_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017186-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 1531, <a href="/wiki/William_Tracy_(JP)" title="William Tracy (JP)">William Tracy</a> was posthumously convicted of heresy for denying purgatory and affirming justification by faith, and his corpse was disinterred and burned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017188_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017188-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Protestants were only a small portion of the population, the growing rift between the King and papacy gave Protestants a new sense of confidence. Heretical ideas were openly discussed, and militant <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> was seen in Essex and Suffolk between 1531 and 1532.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017189\xe2\x80\x93190_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017189\xe2\x80\x93190-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Because they tended to oppose royal supremacy, traditionalists were on the defensive, while Protestants found opportunities to form new alliances with government officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017203\xe2\x80\x93204_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017203\xe2\x80\x93204-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Henrician_Reformation">Henrician Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Henrician Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moderate_reform">Moderate reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Moderate reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/220px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/330px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/440px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3129" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a> (1489\xe2\x80\x931556), Henry VIII\'s Archbishop of Canterbury and editor and co-author of the first and second <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Books of Common Prayer</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In order to promote and defend the Royal Supremacy, Henry VIII embraced the language of the continental Reformation and relied on men with Protestant sympathies, such as Cromwell and Cranmer, to carry out his religious program. The King\'s personal religious views, however, remained largely traditional. Historian <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bernard" title="George W. Bernard">G. W. Bernard</a> writes that Henry\'s religious policies were purposely ambiguous in order to foster religious compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>By 1529, Henry VIII was using the language of reform openly\xe2\x80\x94speaking of Rome\'s "vain and superfluous ceremonies" and blaming the papacy for numerous wars and heresies. At Anne Boleyn\'s urging, he was also reading Protestant books, such as <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fish" title="Simon Fish">Simon Fish</a>\'s <i>Supplication for the Beggars</i> and Tyndale\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Obedience_of_a_Christian_Man" title="The Obedience of a Christian Man">The Obedience of a Christian Man</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> After Warham\'s death, Cranmer was made Archbishop of Canterbury (with papal consent) in 1533.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017205_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017205-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Cranmer\'s shift to Protestantism was borne partly by his membership of the team negotiating for the annulment, finally came through his stay with <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Osiander" title="Andreas Osiander">Andreas Osiander</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> in 1532. (Cranmer also secretly married Osiander\'s niece.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch199669_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch199669-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Even then the position was complicated by the fact that Lutherans were not in favour of the annulment. Cranmer (and Henry) felt obliged to seek assistance from <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>, which brought him into contact with the more radical ideas associated with <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1534, a new Heresy Act ensured that no one could be punished for speaking against the pope and also made it more difficult to convict someone of heresy; however, <a href="/wiki/Sacramentarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacramentarian">sacramentarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anabaptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a> continued to be vigorously persecuted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017208,221_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017208,221-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> What followed was a period of doctrinal confusion as both conservatives and reformers attempted to shape the church\'s future direction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017238_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017238-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The reformers were aided by Thomas Cromwell. In January 1535, the King made Cromwell his <a href="/wiki/Vicegerent" title="Vicegerent">vicegerent</a> in spirituals. Effectively the King\'s <a href="/wiki/Vicar_general" title="Vicar general">vicar general</a>, Cromwell\'s authority was greater than that of bishops. Even the Archbishop of Canterbury answered to Cromwell.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017215_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017215-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Largely due to Anne Boleyn\'s influence, a number of Protestants were appointed bishops between 1534 and 1536. These included Latimer, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Goodrich" title="Thomas Goodrich">Thomas Goodrich</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Salcot" class="mw-redirect" title="John Salcot">John Salcot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Shaxton" title="Nicholas Shaxton">Nicholas Shaxton</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Barlow_(bishop_of_Chichester)" title="William Barlow (bishop of Chichester)">William Barlow</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Hilsey" title="John Hilsey">John Hilsey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Foxe" title="Edward Foxe">Edward Foxe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017216\xe2\x80\x93217_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017216\xe2\x80\x93217-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Protestant bishops remained a minority, however.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017218_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017218-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cromwell\'s programme, assisted by Anne Boleyn\'s influence over episcopal appointments, was not merely against the clergy and the power of Rome. He persuaded Henry that safety from political alliances that Rome might attempt to bring together lay in negotiations with the German Lutheran princes of the <a href="/wiki/Schmalkaldic_League" title="Schmalkaldic League">Schmalkaldic League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> There also seemed to be a possibility that Charles V, the Holy Roman Emperor, might act to avenge his rejected aunt (Queen Catherine) and enforce the Pope\'s excommunication. The negotiations did not lead to an alliance, but it brought Lutheran ideas to England.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993125_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993125-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1536, Convocation adopted the first doctrinal statement for the Church of England, the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-nine_Articles#Ten_Articles_(1536)" title="Thirty-nine Articles">Ten Articles</a>. This was followed by the <i><a href="/wiki/Thirty-nine_Articles#Bishops&#39;_Book_(1537)" title="Thirty-nine Articles">Bishops\' Book</a></i> in 1537. These established a semi-Lutheran doctrine for the church. Justification by faith, qualified by an emphasis on good works following justification, was a core teaching. The traditional seven <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacraments</a> were reduced to three only\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a>. Catholic teaching on <a href="/wiki/Intercession_of_saints" title="Intercession of saints">praying to saints</a>, purgatory and the use of <a href="/wiki/Religious_images_in_Christian_theology" title="Religious images in Christian theology">images</a> in worship was undermined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017254\xe2\x80\x93256_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017254\xe2\x80\x93256-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StPaul%27sCross.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/StPaul%27sCross.jpg/220px-StPaul%27sCross.jpg" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/StPaul%27sCross.jpg/330px-StPaul%27sCross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/StPaul%27sCross.jpg 2x" data-file-width="357" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StPaul%27sCross.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>St Paul\'s Cross (in the lower left corner of the painting) was a prominent <a href="/wiki/Preaching_cross" title="Preaching cross">preaching cross</a> on the grounds of <a href="/wiki/Old_St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="Old St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">Old St Paul\'s Cathedral</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In August 1536, the same month the Ten Articles were published, Cromwell issued a set of Royal Injunctions to the clergy. Minor <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">feast days</a> were changed into normal work days, including those celebrating a <a href="/wiki/Dedications_in_the_Church_of_England" title="Dedications in the Church of England">church\'s patron saint</a> and most feasts during harvest time (July through September). The rationale was partly economic as too many holidays led to a loss of productivity and were "the occasion of vice and idleness".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993129_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993129-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Protestants considered feast days to be examples of superstition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017241_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017241-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Clergy were to discourage <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimages</a> and instruct the people to give to the poor rather than make offerings to images. The clergy were also ordered to place Bibles in both English and Latin in every church for the people to read.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993130_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993130-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> This last requirement was largely ignored by the bishops for a year or more due the lack of any authorised English translation. The only complete vernacular version was the <a href="/wiki/Coverdale_Bible" title="Coverdale Bible">Coverdale Bible</a> finished in 1535 and based on Tyndale\'s earlier work. It lacked royal approval, however.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017241\xe2\x80\x93242_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017241\xe2\x80\x93242-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Diarmaid_MacCulloch" title="Diarmaid MacCulloch">Diarmaid MacCulloch</a> in his study of <i>The Later Reformation in England, 1547-1603</i> argues that after 1537, "England\'s Reformation was characterized by its hatred of images, as <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Aston" title="Margaret Aston">Margaret Aston</a>\'s work on iconoclasm and <a href="/wiki/Iconophobia" title="Iconophobia">iconophobia</a> has repeatedly and eloquently demonstrated."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In February 1538, the famous <a href="/wiki/Rood_of_Grace" title="Rood of Grace">Rood of Grace</a> was condemned as a mechanical fraud and destroyed at <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cross" title="St Paul&#39;s Cross">St Paul\'s Cross</a>. In July, the statues of <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Walsingham" title="Our Lady of Walsingham">Our Lady of Walsingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Our_Lady_of_Ipswich" title="Our Lady of Ipswich">Our Lady of Ipswich</a>, and other Marian images were burned at <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a> on Cromwell\'s orders. In September, Cromwell issued a second set of Royal Injunctions ordering the destruction of images to which pilgrimage offerings were made, the prohibition of lighting candles before images of saints, and the preaching of sermons against the veneration of images and relics. He once again instructed each parish to acquire an English Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993134_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993134-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissolution_of_the_monasteries">Dissolution of the monasteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dissolution of the monasteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fichale.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Fichale.jpg/220px-Fichale.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Fichale.jpg/330px-Fichale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Fichale.jpg/440px-Fichale.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="571" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fichale.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains of <a href="/wiki/Finchale_Priory" title="Finchale Priory">Finchale Priory</a>, a Benedictine monastery near Durham that was closed in 1535</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a></div>\n<p>For Cromwell and Cranmer, a step in the Protestant agenda was attacking <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">monasticism</a>, which was associated with the doctrine of purgatory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993131_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993131-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> While the King was not opposed to <a href="/wiki/Religious_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious house">religious houses</a> on theological grounds, there was concern over the loyalty of the monastic orders, which were international in character and resistant to the Royal Supremacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018226_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018226-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Friars_Minor" title="Order of Friars Minor">Franciscan Observant</a> houses were closed in August 1534 after that order refused to repudiate papal authority. Between 1535 and 1537, 18 <a href="/wiki/Carthusian" class="mw-redirect" title="Carthusian">Carthusians</a> were killed for doing the same.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993141_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993141-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  \n</p><p>The Crown was also experiencing financial difficulties, and the wealth of the church, in contrast to its political weakness, made confiscation of church property both tempting and feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Seizure of monastic wealth was not unprecedented; it had happened before in 1295, 1337, and 1369.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993131_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993131-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Church owned between one-fifth and one-third of the land in all England; Cromwell realised that he could bind the <a href="/wiki/Landed_gentry" title="Landed gentry">gentry</a> and nobility to Royal Supremacy by selling to them the huge amount of Church lands, and that any reversion to pre-Royal Supremacy would entail upsetting many of the powerful people in the realm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElton1991142_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElton1991142-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>In 1534, Cromwell initiated a <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries#Visitation_of_the_monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">visitation of the monasteries</a> ostensibly to examine their character, but in fact, to value their assets with a view to expropriation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1938vii_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1938vii-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The visiting commissioners claimed to have uncovered sexual immorality and financial impropriety amongst the <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a>, which became the ostensible justification for their suppression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElton1991142_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElton1991142-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> There were also reports of the possession and display of false relics, such as <a href="/wiki/Hailes_Abbey" title="Hailes Abbey">Hailes Abbey</a>\'s vial of the <a href="/wiki/Blood_of_Christ" title="Blood of Christ">Holy Blood</a>, upon investigation announced to be "honey clarified and coloured with saffron".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018269_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018269-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Compendium_Competorum" title="Compendium Competorum">Compendium Competorum</a></i> compiled by the visitors documented ten pieces of the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a>, seven portions of the <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Virgin Mary</a>\'s milk and numerous saints\' girdles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018229_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018229-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Leading reformers, led by Anne Boleyn, wanted to convert monasteries into "places of study and good letters, and to the continual relief of the poor", but this was not done.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018232_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2018232-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In 1536, the <a href="/wiki/Suppression_of_Religious_Houses_Act_1535" title="Suppression of Religious Houses Act 1535">Dissolution of the Lesser Monasteries Act</a> closed smaller houses valued at less than \xc2\xa3200 a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993130_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993130-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Henry used the revenue to help build coastal defences (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Device_Forts" title="Device Forts">Device Forts</a></i>) against expected invasion, and all the land was given to the Crown or sold to the aristocracy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Coastal defences are not mentioned in Haigh. (September 2018)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Thirty-four houses were saved by paying for exemptions. Monks and nuns affected by closures were transferred to larger houses, and monks had the option of becoming <a href="/wiki/Secular_clergy" title="Secular clergy">secular clergy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993144\xe2\x80\x93145_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993144\xe2\x80\x93145-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Chapel,_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/The_Chapel%2C_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg/220px-The_Chapel%2C_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/The_Chapel%2C_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg/330px-The_Chapel%2C_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/The_Chapel%2C_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg/440px-The_Chapel%2C_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Chapel,_Forde_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1287934.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chapter_house" title="Chapter house">chapter house</a> of <a href="/wiki/Forde_Abbey" title="Forde Abbey">Forde Abbey</a>, a Cistercian monastery closed in 1539 and converted into a country house</div></div></div>\n<p>The Royal Supremacy and the abolition of papal authority had not caused widespread unrest, but the attacks on monasteries and the abolition of saints\' days and pilgrimages  provoked violence. Mobs attacked those sent to break up monastic buildings. Suppression commissioners were attacked by local people in several places.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993143\xe2\x80\x93144_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993143\xe2\x80\x93144-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">Northern England</a>, there were a series of uprisings against the dissolutions in late 1536 and early 1537. The <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_Rising" class="mw-redirect" title="Lincolnshire Rising">Lincolnshire Rising</a> occurred in October 1536 and culminated in a force of 40,000 rebels assembling at <a href="/wiki/Lincoln,_England" title="Lincoln, England">Lincoln</a>. They demanded an end to taxation during peacetime, the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_uses" class="mw-redirect" title="Statute of uses">statute of uses</a>, an end to the suppression of monasteries, and that heresy be purged and heretics punished. Henry refused to negotiate, and the revolt collapsed as the nervous gentry convinced the common people to disperse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993145\xe2\x80\x93146_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993145\xe2\x80\x93146-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_of_Grace" title="Pilgrimage of Grace">Pilgrimage of Grace</a> was a more serious matter. The revolt began in October at <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a> and spread to the other northern counties. Around 50,000 strong, the rebels under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Aske_(political_leader)" title="Robert Aske (political leader)">Robert Aske</a>\'s leadership restored 16 of the 26 northern monasteries that had been dissolved. Due to the size of the rebellion, the King was persuaded to negotiate. In December, the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_3rd_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 3rd Duke of Norfolk">Duke of Norfolk</a> offered the rebels a pardon and a parliament to consider their grievances. Aske then sent the rebels home. The promises made to them, however, were ignored by the King, and Norfolk was instructed to put the rebellion down. Forty-seven of the Lincolnshire rebels were executed, and 132 from the Pilgrimage of Grace. In <a href="/wiki/Southern_England" title="Southern England">Southern England</a>, smaller disturbances took place in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walsingham" title="Walsingham">Walsingham</a> in 1537.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993147\xe2\x80\x93149_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993147\xe2\x80\x93149-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The failure of the Pilgrimage of Grace only sped up the process of dissolution and may have convinced Henry VIII that all religious houses needed to be closed. In 1540, the last monasteries were dissolved, wiping out an important element of traditional religion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003201_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003201-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Henry personally devised a plan to form at least thirteen new dioceses so that most counties had one based on a former monastery (or more than one), though this scheme was only partly carried out.
