New dioceses were established at <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Westminster_(Church_of_England)" title="Diocese of Westminster (Church of England)">Westminster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chester" title="Chester">Chester</a>, but not, for instance, at <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">Leicester</a> or <a href="/wiki/Waltham_Abbey_(abbey)" class="mw-redirect" title="Waltham Abbey (abbey)">Waltham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_reversed">Reformation reversed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reformation reversed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to historian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Marshall_(historian)" title="Peter Marshall (historian)">Peter Marshall</a>, Henry\'s religious reforms were based on the principles of "unity, obedience and the refurbishment of ancient truth."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017266_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017266-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Yet, the outcome was disunity and disobedience. Impatient Protestants took it upon themselves to further reform. Priests said mass in English rather than Latin and were marrying in violation of <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy" title="Clerical celibacy">clerical celibacy</a>. Not only were their divisions between traditionalists and reformers. Protestants themselves were divided between establishment reformers who held Lutheran beliefs and radicals who held Anabaptist and Sacramentarian views.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017269\xe2\x80\x93270_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017269\xe2\x80\x93270-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Reports of dissension from every part of England reached Cromwell daily\xe2\x80\x94developments he tried to hide from the King.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000132_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000132-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>In September 1538, Bishop <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gardiner" title="Stephen Gardiner">Stephen Gardiner</a>, a leading conservative, returned to England from three years in France. Almost immediately, official religious policy began to drift in a conservative direction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996229_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996229-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> This was due in part to the eagerness of establishment Protestants to disassociate themselves from religious radicals. In September, two Lutheran princes, the <a href="/wiki/John_Frederick_I,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="John Frederick I, Elector of Saxony">Elector of Saxony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse" title="Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse">Landgrave of Hesse</a>, sent warnings of Anabaptist activity in England. A commission was swiftly created to seek out Anabaptists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996231_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996231-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Henry personally presided at the trial of <a href="/wiki/John_Lambert_(Protestant_martyr)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Lambert (Protestant martyr)">John Lambert</a> in November 1538 for denying the <a href="/wiki/Real_presence" class="mw-redirect" title="Real presence">real presence</a> of Christ in the Eucharist. At the same time, he shared in the drafting of a proclamation ordering Anabaptists and Sacramentaries to get out of the country or face death. Discussion of the real presence (except by those educated in the universities) was forbidden, and priests who married were to be dismissed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000132_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000132-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996233_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996233-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> It was becoming clear that the King\'s views on religion differed from those of Cromwell and Cranmer. Henry made his traditional preferences known during the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Triduum" class="mw-redirect" title="Easter Triduum">Easter Triduum</a> of 1539, where he crept to the cross on <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Good Friday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996241_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996241-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Later that year, Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Six_Articles_(1539)" class="mw-redirect" title="Six Articles (1539)">Six Articles</a> reaffirming Roman Catholic beliefs and practices such as <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a>, clerical celibacy, <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confession</a> to a priest, <a href="/wiki/Votive_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Votive mass">votive masses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Communion_under_both_kinds" title="Communion under both kinds">communion in one kind</a> (that is withholding <a href="/wiki/Communion_wine" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion wine">communion wine</a> from the laity).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993153_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993153-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 June 1540 Cromwell, Henry\'s longtime advisor and loyal servant, was executed. Different reasons were advanced: that Cromwell would not enforce the Act of Six Articles; that he had supported Barnes, Latimer and other heretics; and that he was responsible for Henry\'s marriage to <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Cleves" title="Anne of Cleves">Anne of Cleves</a>, his fourth wife. Many other arrests under the Act followed. Cranmer lay low.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000135_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000135-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chantry_Chapel,_Wakefield_Bridge_(7568910296).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chantry_Chapel%2C_Wakefield_Bridge_%287568910296%29.jpg/220px-Chantry_Chapel%2C_Wakefield_Bridge_%287568910296%29.jpg" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chantry_Chapel%2C_Wakefield_Bridge_%287568910296%29.jpg/330px-Chantry_Chapel%2C_Wakefield_Bridge_%287568910296%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chantry_Chapel%2C_Wakefield_Bridge_%287568910296%29.jpg/440px-Chantry_Chapel%2C_Wakefield_Bridge_%287568910296%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2634" data-file-height="3240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chantry_Chapel,_Wakefield_Bridge_(7568910296).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Chantry_Chapel_of_St_Mary_the_Virgin,_Wakefield" title="Chantry Chapel of St Mary the Virgin, Wakefield">Chantry Chapel of St Mary the Virgin</a> in Wakefield, West Yorkshire. Chantries were endowments that paid priests to say <a href="/wiki/Requiem" title="Requiem">masses for the dead</a> to lessen their time in purgatory.</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite such setbacks, Protestants managed to win some victories. In May 1541, the King ordered copies of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Bible" title="Great Bible">Great Bible</a> to be placed in all churches; failure to comply would result in a \xc2\xa32 fine. Protestants could celebrate the growing access to vernacular scripture as most churches had Bibles by 1545. The King also continued to endorse the iconoclastic policies of 1538 and was angry over the North\'s continued attachment to shrines and pilgrimage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017284_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017284-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993158_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993158-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, Cranmer survived formal charges of heresy in the <a href="/wiki/Prebendaries%27_Plot" title="Prebendaries&#39; Plot">Prebendaries\' Plot</a> of 1543.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017286\xe2\x80\x93287_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017286\xe2\x80\x93287-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Traditionalists, nevertheless, seemed to have the upper hand. In May 1543, a new formulary was published to replace the <i>Bishops\' Book</i>. This <i><a href="/wiki/King%27s_Book" class="mw-redirect" title="King&#39;s Book">King\'s Book</a></i> rejected justification by faith alone and defended traditional ceremonies and the use of images.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993160_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993160-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> This was followed days later by passage of the <a href="/wiki/Act_for_the_Advancement_of_True_Religion" title="Act for the Advancement of True Religion">Act for the Advancement of True Religion</a>, which restricted Bible reading to men and women of noble birth. Henry expressed his fears to Parliament in 1545 that "the Word of God, is disputed, rhymed, sung and jangled in every ale house and tavern, contrary to the true meaning and doctrine of the same."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> By the spring of 1543, Protestant innovations had been reversed. Only the break with Rome and the dissolution of the monasteries remained unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993161_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993161-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>By the spring of 1544, the conservatives appeared to be losing influence once again. In March, Parliament made it more difficult to prosecute people for violating the Six Articles. Protestants achieved small victories with the authorisation of Cranmer\'s English <a href="/wiki/Litany" title="Litany">Litany</a> in June 1544 and a <i>King\'s Primer</i> in May 1545, which became the only authorised prayer book in England.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> After the death of the conservative <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lee_(bishop)" title="Edward Lee (bishop)">Edward Lee</a> in September 1544, the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Robert_Holgate" title="Robert Holgate">Robert Holgate</a> replaced him as Archbishop of York.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017292_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017292-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In December 1545, the King was empowered to seize the property of <a href="/wiki/Chantries" class="mw-redirect" title="Chantries">chantries</a> (endowments created to provide <a href="/wiki/Requiem" title="Requiem">masses for the dead</a>). While Henry\'s motives were largely financial (England was at war with France and desperately in need of funds), the passage of the Chantries Act was "an indication of how deeply the doctrine of purgatory had been eroded and discredited".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017294_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall2017294-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1546, the conservatives were once again in the ascendent. A series of controversial sermons preached by the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Edward_Crome" title="Edward Crome">Edward Crome</a> set off a persecution of Protestants that the traditionalists used to effectively target their rivals. It was during this time that <a href="/wiki/Anne_Askew" title="Anne Askew">Anne Askew</a> was tortured in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> and burnt at the stake. Even Henry\'s last wife, <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Parr" class="mw-redirect" title="Katherine Parr">Katherine Parr</a>, was suspected of heresy but saved herself by appealing to the King\'s mercy. With the Protestants on the defensive, traditionalists pressed their advantange by banning Protestant books.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993165\xe2\x80\x93166_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993165\xe2\x80\x93166-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The conservative persecution of Queen Katherine, however, backfired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996356_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996356-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> By November 1546, there were already signs that religious policy was once again tilting towards Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The King\'s will provided for a <a href="/wiki/Regency" class="mw-redirect" title="Regency">regency</a> council to rule after his death, which would have been dominated by traditionalists, such as the Duke of Norfolk, Lord Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wriothesley,_1st_Earl_of_Southampton" title="Thomas Wriothesley, 1st Earl of Southampton">Wriothesly</a>, Bishop Gardiner and Bishop <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert_Tunstall" title="Cuthbert Tunstall">Tunstall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993166_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993166-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> After a dispute with the King, Bishop Gardiner, the leading conservative churchman, was disgraced and removed as a councilor. Later, the Duke of Norfolk, the most powerful conservative nobleman, was arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996359_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996359-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> By the time Henry died in 1547, the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour</a>, brother of <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Jane Seymour</a>, Henry\'s third wife (and therefore uncle to the future Edward VI), managed\xe2\x80\x94by a number of alliances with influential Protestants such as <a href="/wiki/John_Dudley" class="mw-redirect" title="John Dudley">Lord Lisle</a>\xe2\x80\x94to gain control over the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993166\xe2\x80\x93167_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993166\xe2\x80\x93167-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Edward.27s_Reformation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward\'s_Reformation">Edward\'s Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Edward&#039;s Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg/220px-Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg/330px-Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg/440px-Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg 2x" data-file-width="711" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Edward_VI_of_England.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King Edward VI of England, in whose reign the reform of the Anglican Church moved in a more Protestant direction.</div></div></div>\n<p>When Henry died in 1547, his nine-year-old son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a>, inherited the throne. Edward was a precocious child who had been brought up as a Protestant, but was initially of little account politically.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour</a> was made Lord Protector. He was commissioned as virtual regent with near sovereign powers. Now made <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Somerset" title="Duke of Somerset">Duke of Somerset</a>, he proceeded at first hesitantly, partly because his powers were not unchallenged. When he acted it was because he saw the political advantage in doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993169_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993169-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1547 injunctions against images were a more tightly drawn version of those of 1538, but they were more fiercely enforced, at first informally, and then by instruction. All images in churches were to be dismantled. <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">Stained glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrines" class="mw-redirect" title="Shrines">shrines</a> and statues were defaced or destroyed. <a href="/wiki/Rood" title="Rood">Roods</a>, and often their lofts and screens, were cut down and bells were taken down. <a href="/wiki/Vestments" class="mw-redirect" title="Vestments">Vestments</a> were prohibited and either burned or sold. <a href="/wiki/Chalice_(cup)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chalice (cup)">Chalices</a> were melted down or sold.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy480-485_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy480-485-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The requirement of the clergy to be <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy" title="Clerical celibacy">celibate</a> was lifted. Processions were banned and ashes and palms were prohibited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2005461_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2005461-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chantry" title="Chantry">Chantries</a> (endowments to provide masses for the dead) were abolished completely. How well this was received is disputed. Modern historian <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Geoffrey_Dickens" title="Arthur Geoffrey Dickens">A.G. Dickens</a> contends that people had "...ceased to believe in intercessory masses for souls in purgatory",<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> while others, such as <a href="/wiki/Eamon_Duffy" title="Eamon Duffy">Eamon Duffy</a>, argue that the demolition of chantry chapels and the removal of images coincided with the activity of royal visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2005481_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2005481-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The evidence is often ambiguous.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy481_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy481-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ed_and_pope.png" class="image"><img alt="A painting" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ed_and_pope.png/220px-Ed_and_pope.png" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ed_and_pope.png/330px-Ed_and_pope.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ed_and_pope.png/440px-Ed_and_pope.png 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1054" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ed_and_pope.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Edward VI and the Pope: An Allegory of the Reformation</i>. This <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan</a> work of propaganda depicts the handing over of power from Henry VIII, who lies dying in bed, to Edward VI, seated beneath a cloth of state with a slumping pope at his feet. In the top right of the picture is an image of men pulling down and smashing idols. At Edward\'s side are his uncle the Lord Protector <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour</a> and members of the Privy Council.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1549 Cranmer introduced a <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> in English, which while to all appearances kept the structure of the Mass, altered the theology so that the holy gifts of consecrated bread and wine were not offered to God as a sacrifice although he was well aware that this had been the Church\'s doctrine since the late 2nd century (it would be restored by Scottish non-Jurors of the Episcopal Church of Scotland and the Protestant Episcopal Church of the United States in 1789)   In 1550 stone altars were replaced by wooden communion tables, a very public break with the past, as it changed the look and focus of church interiors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2005472_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2005472-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Less visible, but still influential, was the new ordinal\xe2\x80\x94which provided for Protestant ministers rather than Roman Catholic priests, an admittedly conservative adaptation of Bucer\'s draft;<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> its Preface explicitly mentions the historic succession but it has been described as "... another case of Cranmer\'s opportunist adoption of medieval forms for new purposes."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch1996-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In 1551, the episcopate was remodelled by the appointment of Protestants to the bench. This removed the refusal of some bishops to enforce the regulations as an obstacle to change.\n</p><p>Henceforth, the Reformation proceeded apace. In 1552, the prayer book\xe2\x80\x94which the conservative Bishop <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gardiner" title="Stephen Gardiner">Stephen Gardiner</a> had approved from his prison cell as being "patient of a Catholic interpretation"\xe2\x80\x94was replaced by a second, much more radical prayer book that altered the service to remove any sense that the Eucharist was a material sacrifice offered to God while keeping the belief that it was a sacrifice of thanksgiving and praise (in word). Edward\'s Parliament also repealed his father\'s Six Articles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The enforcement of the new liturgy did not always take place without a struggle. Conformity was the order of the day, but in <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> there were <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Prayer Book rebellion">rebellions</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> as also in Cornwall, to which many parishes sent their young men; they were put down only after considerable loss of life. In other places the causes of the rebellions were less easy to pin down,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> but by July throughout southern England, there was "quavering quiet," which burst out into "stirs" in many places, most significantly in <a href="/wiki/Kett%27s_Rebellion" title="Kett&#39;s Rebellion">Kett\'s Rebellion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">Norwich</a>.\n</p><p>Apart from these more spectacular pieces of resistance, in some places chantry priests continued to say prayers and landowners to pay them to do so. Opposition to the removal of images was widespread\xe2\x80\x94so much so that when during the Commonwealth, <a href="/wiki/William_Dowsing" title="William Dowsing">William Dowsing</a> was commissioned to the task of image breaking in <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>, his task, as he records it, was enormous.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> and the southeast, compliance was mostly willing and for many, the sale of vestments and plate was an opportunity to make money (but it was also true that in London and Kent, Reformation ideas had permeated more deeply into popular thinking).\n</p><p>The effect of the resistance was to topple Somerset as Lord Protector, so that in 1549 it was feared by some that the Reformation would cease. The prayer book was the tipping point. But Lisle, now made Earl of Warwick, was made Lord President of the Privy Council and, ever the opportunist (he died a public Roman Catholic), he saw the further implementation of the reforming policy as a means of defeating his rivals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993176_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993176-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg/200px-S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg" width="200" height="356" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg/300px-S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg/400px-S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:S95ReformationDestructionEly.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reformation-Vandalized statue in the Lady Chapel of <a href="/wiki/Ely_Cathedral" title="Ely Cathedral">Ely Cathedral</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Outwardly, the destruction and removals for sale had changed the church forever. Many churches concealed their vestments and their silver,<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy490_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy490-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and had buried their stone altars. There were many disputes between the government and parishes over church property. Thus, when Edward died in July 1553 and the Duke of Northumberland attempted to have the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a> made Queen, the unpopularity of the confiscations gave Mary the opportunity to have herself proclaimed Queen, first in Suffolk, and then in London to the acclamation of the crowds.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marian_Restoration">Marian Restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Marian Restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mary_I_of_England.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mary_I_of_England.jpg/220px-Mary_I_of_England.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mary_I_of_England.jpg/330px-Mary_I_of_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mary_I_of_England.jpg/440px-Mary_I_of_England.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2237" data-file-height="2952" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mary_I_of_England.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen Mary I of England restored the English allegiance to Rome.</div></div></div>\n<p>From 1553, under the reign of Henry\'s Roman Catholic daughter, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a>, the Reformation legislation was repealed and Mary sought to achieve the reunion with Rome. Her first Act of Parliament was to <a href="/wiki/Ex_post_facto_law" title="Ex post facto law">retroactively</a> validate Henry\'s marriage to her mother and so legitimise her claim to the throne.\n</p><p>Achieving her objective was, however, not straightforward. Before reunion with Rome could occur, church property disputes had to be settled\xe2\x80\x94which, in practice, meant letting those who had bought former church property keep it. Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Pole" title="Reginald Pole">Reginald Pole</a> arrived in November 1554 to become Archbishop of Canterbury in Cranmer\'s place. Mary could have had Cranmer, imprisoned as he was, tried and executed for treason\xe2\x80\x94he had supported the claims of Lady Jane Grey\xe2\x80\x94but she resolved to have him tried for <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>. His recantations of his Protestantism would have been a major coup. Unhappily for her, he unexpectedly withdrew his recantations at the last minute as he was to be burned at the stake, thus ruining her government\'s propaganda victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003281\xe2\x80\x93282_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003281\xe2\x80\x93282-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>If Mary was to secure England for Roman Catholicism, she needed an heir. On the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> she married his son, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II of Spain</a>; she needed to prevent her Protestant half-sister Elizabeth from inheriting the Crown and thus returning England to Protestantism. There was opposition, and even a rebellion in Kent (led by <a href="/wiki/Wyatt%27s_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Wyatt&#39;s Rebellion">Sir Thomas Wyatt</a>); even though it was provided that Philip would never inherit the kingdom if there was no heir, received no estates and had no coronation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003281_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003281-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> He was there to provide an heir. But she never became pregnant, and likely suffered from cancer. Ironically, another blow fell. Pope Julius died and his successor, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_IV" title="Pope Paul IV">Pope Paul IV</a>, declared war on Philip and recalled Pole to Rome to have him tried as a heretic. Mary refused to let him go. The support she might have expected from a grateful Pope was thus denied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003284\xe2\x80\x93285_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003284\xe2\x80\x93285-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>After 1555, the initial reconciling tone of the regime began to harden. The medieval heresy laws were restored. The <a href="/wiki/Marian_Persecutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Marian Persecutions">Marian Persecutions</a> of Protestants ensued and 283 Protestants were burnt at the stake for heresy. This resulted in the Queen becoming known as <i>Bloody Mary</i>, due to the influence of <a href="/wiki/John_Foxe" title="John Foxe">John Foxe</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Marian_exiles" title="Marian exiles">Protestants who fled</a> Marian England. <a href="/wiki/Foxe%27s_Book_of_Martyrs" title="Foxe&#39;s Book of Martyrs">Foxe\'s <i>Book of Martyrs</i></a> recorded the executions in such detail that it became Mary\'s epitaph; <a href="/wiki/Convocation_of_the_English_Clergy" class="mw-redirect" title="Convocation of the English Clergy">Convocation</a> subsequently ordered that Foxe\'s book should be placed in every cathedral in the land. In fact, while those who were executed after the revolts of 1536, and the St David\'s Down rebellion of 1549, and the unknown number of monks who died for refusing to submit, may not have been tried for heresy, they certainly exceeded that number by some amount. Even so, the heroism of some of the martyrs was an example to those who witnessed them, so that in some places it was the burnings that set people against the regime.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There was a slow consolidation in Roman Catholic strength in Mary\'s latter years. The reconciled Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Bonner" title="Edmund Bonner">Edmund Bonner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_London" title="Bishop of London">Bishop of London</a>, produced a catechism and a collection of homilies. Printing presses produced primers and other devotional materials, and recruitment to the English clergy began to rise after almost a decade. Repairs to long-neglected churches began. In the parishes "...restoration and repair continued, new bells were bought, and churches\' ales produced their bucolic profits."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993234_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993234-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Commissioners visited to ensure that altars were restored, roods rebuilt and vestments and plate purchased. Moreover, Pole was determined to do more than remake the past. He insisted on scripture, teaching and education, and on improving the clergy\'s moral standards.\n</p><p>It is difficult to determine how far previous reigns had broken Roman Catholic devotion, with its belief in the saints and in purgatory, but certainties\xe2\x80\x94especially those that drew public financial support\xe2\x80\x94had been shaken. <a href="/wiki/Benefaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Benefaction">Benefactions</a> to the church did not return significantly. Trust in clergy who had changed their minds and were now willing to leave their new wives\xe2\x80\x94as they were required to do\xe2\x80\x94was bound to have weakened.\n</p><p>Few monasteries, <a href="/wiki/Chantry" title="Chantry">chantries</a>, and guilds were reinstated. "Parish religion was marked by religious and cultural sterility,"<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> though some have observed enthusiasm, marred only by poor harvests that produced poverty and want.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993214_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993214-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Full restoration of the Roman Catholic faith in England to its pre-Reformation state would take time. Consequently, Protestants secretly ministering to underground congregations, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bentham" title="Thomas Bentham">Thomas Bentham</a>, were planning for a long haul, a ministry of survival.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993235_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993235-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Mary\'s death in November 1558, childless and without having made provision for a Roman Catholic to succeed her, would undo her consolidation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elizabethan_Settlement">Elizabethan Settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Elizabethan Settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(March 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a></div>\n<p>Following Mary\'s childless death, her half-sister <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a> inherited the throne. One of the most important concerns during Elizabeth\'s early reign was <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">religion</a>. Elizabeth could not be Roman Catholic, as that church considered her illegitimate. At the same time, she had observed the turmoil brought about by Edward\'s introduction of radical Protestant reforms. Communion with the Roman Catholic Church was again severed by Elizabeth. She relied primarily on her chief advisors, Sir William Cecil, as her Secretary of State, and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Bacon_(courtier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicholas Bacon (courtier)">Sir Nicholas Bacon</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Keeper_of_the_Great_Seal" title="Lord Keeper of the Great Seal">Lord Keeper of the Great Seal</a>, for direction on the matter. Chiefly she supported her father\'s idea of reforming the church but made some minor adjustments. In this way, Elizabeth and her advisors aimed at a church that included most opinions.\n</p><p>Two groups were excluded. Roman Catholics who remained loyal to the Pope after he excommunicated the Queen in 1570 would not be tolerated. They were, in fact, regarded as traitors, because the Pope had refused to accept Elizabeth as Queen of England. Roman Catholics were given the hard choice of being loyal either to their church or their country. For some priests it meant life on the run, in some cases death for treason.\n</p><p>The other group not to be tolerated was people who wanted reform to go much further, and who finally gave up on the Church of England. They could no longer see it as a true church. They believed it had refused to obey the Bible, so they formed small groups of convinced believers outside the church. The government responded with imprisonment and exile to try to crush these "separatists".\n</p><p>The Church of England itself contained three groups. Those who believed the form of the church was just what it should be included leaders like John Jewel and Richard Hooker. A second group looked for opportunities to reintroduce some Roman Catholic practices. Under the Stuart kings they would have their chance. The third group, who came to be called <i>Puritans</i>, wanted to remove remaining traces of the old ways. The Stuart kings were to give them a rough passage. At the end of Elizabeth\'s reign, the Church of England was firmly in place, but held the seeds of future conflict.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Darnley_stage_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Darnley_stage_3.jpg/220px-Darnley_stage_3.jpg" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Darnley_stage_3.jpg/330px-Darnley_stage_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Darnley_stage_3.jpg/440px-Darnley_stage_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1581" data-file-height="2313" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Darnley_stage_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen Elizabeth I of England reached a moderate religious settlement.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a> was summoned in 1559 to consider the Reformation Bill and to create a new church. The Reformation Bill defined the <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)#Communion_rite" title="Mass (liturgy)">Communion</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Consubstantiation" title="Consubstantiation">consubstantial</a> celebration as opposed to a <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantial</a> celebration, included abuse of the pope in the <a href="/wiki/Litany" title="Litany">litany</a>, and ordered that ministers (meaning ordained clergy) should not wear the <a href="/wiki/Surplice" title="Surplice">surplice</a> or other Roman Catholic vestments. It allowed the clergy \xe2\x80\x93 deacons, priests and bishops \xe2\x80\x93 to marry, banned images from churches, and confirmed Elizabeth as Supreme Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.\n</p><p>The Bill met heavy resistance in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, as Roman Catholic bishops as well as the lay peers voted against it. They reworked much of the Bill, changed the litany to allow for a transubstantial belief in the Communion and refused to grant Elizabeth the title of Supreme Head of the Church. Parliament was <a href="/wiki/Prorogued" class="mw-redirect" title="Prorogued">prorogued</a> over Easter, and when it resumed, the government entered two new bills into the Houses\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1558" title="Act of Supremacy 1558">Act of Supremacy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1558" title="Act of Uniformity 1558">Act of Uniformity</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Act_of_Supremacy_1558">Act of Supremacy 1558</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Act of Supremacy 1558">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1558" title="Act of Supremacy 1558">This Act</a> made null and void (with certain specific exceptions) the Marian act of 1554 that had repealed all Henry VIII\'s legislation from 1529 onwards, which had denied the authority of the See of Rome<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and also confirmed Elizabeth as Supreme Governor of the Church of England. Supreme Governor was a suitably equivocal title that made Elizabeth head of the Church without ever saying she was. This was important for two reasons: (1) it satisfied those who felt that a woman could not rule the church, and (2) it acted in a conciliatory way toward English Roman Catholics. For the clergy, Elizabeth\'s changes were more wholesale than those of her half-brother, Edward, had been. All but one (<a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kitchin" title="Anthony Kitchin">Anthony Kitchin</a>) of the bishops lost their posts, a hundred fellows of Oxford colleges were deprived; many dignitaries resigned rather than take the oath. The bishops who were removed from the ecclesiastical bench were replaced by appointees who would agree to the reforms. Since the government was concerned that continuity of Orders continue without a break Mathew Parker was consecrated Archbishop of Canterbury by two bishops who had been consecrated in the mid-1530s using the Pontifical and two with the English Ordinal of 1550.\n</p><p>On the question of images, Elizabeth\'s initial reaction was to allow crucifixes and candlesticks and the restoration of roods, but some of the new bishops whom she had elevated protested. In 1560 <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Grindal" title="Edmund Grindal">Edmund Grindal</a>, one of the Marian exiles now made <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_London" title="Bishop of London">Bishop of London</a>, was allowed to enforce the demolition of rood lofts in London and in 1561 the Queen herself ordered the demolition of all lofts.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Thereafter, the determination to prevent any further restoration was evidenced by the more thoroughgoing destruction of <a href="/wiki/Rood" title="Rood">roods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vestments" class="mw-redirect" title="Vestments">vestments</a>, stone altars, <a href="/wiki/Doom_(painting)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doom (painting)">dooms</a>, statues and other ornaments. The queen also appointed a new <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>, removing many Roman Catholic counsellors by doing so. Under Elizabeth, factionalism in the Council and conflicts at court greatly diminished. The Act of Supremacy was passed without difficulty.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Act_of_Uniformity_1558">Act of Uniformity 1558</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Act of Uniformity 1558">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1558" title="Act of Uniformity 1558">Act of Uniformity 1558</a>, which forced people to attend Sunday service in a parish church with a new version of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, passed by only three votes.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The Bill of Uniformity was more cautious than the initial Reformation Bill. It revoked the harsh laws proposed against Roman Catholics, it removed the abuse of the pope from the litany and kept the wording that allowed for both consubstantial and transubstantial interpretations of the presence of Christ in the Eucharist without making any declaration about the matter (transubstantion is actually condemned in the Thirty-Nine Articles).\n</p><p>After Parliament was dismissed, Elizabeth and Cecil drafted the Royal Injunctions. These were additions to the settlement, and largely stressed continuity with the Catholic past \xe2\x80\x93 clergy were ordered to wear the surplice and the use of the cope was allowed in cathedrals and collegiate chapels \xe2\x80\x93 especially since all the clergy upon her accession the throne were Roman Catholic. Men were ordained to the three traditional orders of deacon, priest and bishop and so referred to in the Prayer Book Rites. The Ornaments Rubric states that the ornaments of the church and ministers thereof shall remain as they were in the second year of the reign of Edward VI, i.e. in 1548, when Mass was still celebrated (the Oxford Movement in the 19th century interpreted this as permission to wear chasubles, dalmatics and other vestments). Wafers, as opposed to ordinary baker\'s bread, were to be used as the bread at Communion. Communion would be taken kneeling. The Black Rubric denied the real and essential presence of Christ in the consecrated elements but allowed kneeling as long as this act did not imply adoration. The Queen had it removed. There had been opposition to the settlement in rural England, which for the most part was largely Roman Catholic, so the changes aimed for acceptance of the settlement. What succeeded more than anything else was the sheer length of Elizabeth\'s reign; while Mary had been able to impose her programme for a mere five years, Elizabeth had forty-five. Those who delayed, "looking for a new day" when restoration would again be commanded, were defeated by the passing of years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993245_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993245-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Puritans_and_Roman_Catholics">Puritans and Roman Catholics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Puritans and Roman Catholics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks old nibba" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;border-collapse:collapse;"><tbody><tr><td style="background-color:#B2BEB5;;padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Puritans" title="Category:Puritans">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="background-color:#B2BEB5;;padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Puritan_by_Augustus_Saint-Gaudens_-_Springfield,_Massachusetts_-_DSC02513.JPG" class="image"><img alt="The Puritan by Augustus Saint-Gaudens - Springfield, 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="5472" /></a><div style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/The_Puritan_(Springfield)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Puritan (Springfield)"><i>The Puritan</i></a> (1887), a statue in <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a> by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Saint-Gaudens" title="Augustus Saint-Gaudens">Augustus Saint-Gaudens</a></div></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Background</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">English Reformation</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arminianism" title="Arminianism">Arminianism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arminianism_in_the_Church_of_England" href="/wiki/Independent_(religion)" title="Independent (religion)">Independents</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">Nonconformism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/English_Presbyterianism" title="English Presbyterianism">English Presbyterianism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_separatism" title="Ecclesiastical separatism">Ecclesiastical separatism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/17th-century_denominations_in_England" title="17th-century denominations in England">17th-century denominations in England</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Crucial themes</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_Puritanism" title="Definitions of Puritanism">Definitions of Puritanism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Impropriation" title="Impropriation">Impropriation</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Puritan_Sabbatarianism" title="Puritan choir</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Puritan_work_ethic" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan work ethic">Puritan work ethic</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Merton_thesis" title="Merton thesis">Merton thesis</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Elsewhere</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Troubles_at_Frankfurt" title="Troubles at Frankfurt">Troubles at Frankfurt</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;"><a href="/wiki/List_of_Puritans" title="List of Puritans">Notable individuals</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Peter_Bulkley" title="Peter Bulkley">Peter Bulkley</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan">John Bunyan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/William_Bradford_(Plymouth_Colony_governor)" title="William Bradford (Plymouth Colony governor)">William Bradford</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anne_Bradstreet" title="Anne Bradstreet">Anne Bradstreet</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Cotton_(minister)" title="John Cotton (minister)">John Cotton</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/John_Endecott" title="John Endecott">John Endecott</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Edwards_(theologian)" title="Jonathan Edwards (theologian)">Jonathan Edwards</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anne_Hutchinson" title="Anne Hutchinson">Anne Hutchinson</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cotton_Mather" title="Cotton Mather">Cotton Mather</a></li>\n<li><a Winthrop</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Woodford_(17th-century_diarist)" title="Robert Woodford (17th-century diarist)">Robert Woodford</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Works</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Godly_Man%27s_Picture" title="The Godly Man&#39;s Picture">The Godly Man\'s Picture</a></i></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Pilgrim%27s_Progress" title="The Pilgrim&#39;s Progress">The Pilgrim\'s Progress</a></i></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Foxe%27s_Book_of_Martyrs" title="Foxe&#39;s Book of Martyrs">Foxe\'s Book of Martyrs</a></i></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background-color:#B2BEB5;;background:#B2BEB5;padding:0.2em &#39;&#39;&#39;Bold text&#39;&#39;&#39;;">Continuing movements</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-left:0.2em; padding-right:0.2em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Congregational_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregational churches">Congregational churches</a> (<small><a href="/wiki/Congregationalism_in_the_United_States" title="Congregationalism in the United States">U.S.</a></small>)</li>\n<li>other <a href="/wiki/Reformed_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed churches">Reformed churches</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Fat_nibba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template:Fat nibba (page does not exist)"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Fat_nibba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Fat nibba (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fat_nibba&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Elizabeth\'s reign saw the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritanism</a>, which encompassed those Protestants who, whilst they agreed that there should be one national church, felt that the church had been but partially reformed. Puritanism ranged from hostility to the content of the Prayer Book and "popish" ceremony, to a desire that <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_polity" title="Ecclesiastical polity">church governance</a> be radically reformed. Grindal was made <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> in 1575 and chose to oppose even the Queen in his desire to forward the Puritan agenda. He ended a 6,000-word reproach to her with, "Bear with me, I beseech you Madam, if I choose rather to offend your earthly majesty than to offend the heavenly majesty of God."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003384_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003384-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> He was placed under house arrest for his trouble and though he was not deprived, his death in 1583 put an end to the hopes of his supporters.\n</p><p>Grindal\'s successor, <a href="/wiki/John_Whitgift" title="John Whitgift">Archbishop Whitgift</a>, more reflected the Queen\'s determination to discipline those who were unprepared to accept her settlement. A conformist, he imposed a degree of obedience on the clergy that apparently alarmed even the Queen\'s ministers, such as Lord Burghley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003385_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003385-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The Puritan cause was not helped even by its friends. The pseudonymous "<a href="/wiki/Martin_Marprelate" title="Martin Marprelate">Martin Marprelate</a>" tracts, which attacked conformist clergy with a libellous humorous tone, outraged senior Puritan clergy and set the government on an unsuccessful attempt to run the writer to earth.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> in 1588 incidentally made it more difficult for Puritans to resist the conclusion that since God "blew with his wind and they were scattered" he could not be too offended by the religious establishment in the land.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003387_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003387-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the other side, there were still huge numbers of Roman Catholics. Some conformed, bending with the times, hoping that there would be a fresh reverse. Vestments were still hidden, golden candlesticks bequeathed, chalices kept. The Mass was still celebrated in some places<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993253_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993253-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> alongside the new Communion service but was more difficult than before. Both Roman Catholic priests and laity lived a double life, apparently conforming, but avoiding taking the oath of conformity. Only as time passed did recusancy\xe2\x80\x94refusal to attend Protestant services\xe2\x80\x94became more common. Jesuits and seminary priests, trained in Douai and Rome to make good the losses of English priests, encouraged this.\n</p><p>By the 1570s, an underground church was growing fast as the Church of England became more Protestant and less bearable for Roman Catholics who were still a sizeable minority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993267_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993267-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Only one public attempt to restore the old religion occurred: the <a href="/wiki/Rising_of_the_North" title="Rising of the North">Rising of the Northern earls</a> in 1569. It was a botched attempt; in spite of tumultuous crowds who greeted the rebels in <a href="/wiki/Durham,_England" title="Durham, England">Durham</a>, the rebellion did not spread. The assistance they sought did not materialise, their communication with allies at Court was poor. They came nowhere near to freeing <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary Stuart</a>, whose presence might have rallied support, from her imprisonment in Tutbury.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Roman Catholic Church\'s refusal to countenance occasional attendance at Protestant services, as well as the excommunication of Elizabeth by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> in 1570, presented the choice to Roman Catholics more starkly. The arrival of the seminary priests, while it was a lifeline to many Roman Catholics, brought further trouble. Elizabeth\'s ministers took steps to stem the tide: fines for refusal to attend church were raised from 12&#160;d. per service to \xc2\xa320 a month, fifty times an artisan\'s wage; it was now treason to be absolved from schism and reconciled to Rome; the execution of priests began\xe2\x80\x94the first in 1577, four in 1581, eleven in 1582, two in 1583, six in 1584, fifty-three by 1590, and seventy more between 1601 and 1608.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003392_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacCulloch2003392-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> It became treasonable for a Roman Catholic priest ordained abroad to enter the country. Because the papacy had called for the deposing of the Queen, the choice for moderate Roman Catholics lay between treason and damnation. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Catholic_martyrs_of_the_English_Reformation" title="List of Catholic martyrs of the English Reformation">List of Catholic martyrs of the English Reformation</a> was extensive.\n</p><p>There is some distance between legislation and its enforcement. The governmental attacks on <a href="/wiki/Recusancy" title="Recusancy">recusancy</a> were mostly upon the gentry. Few recusants were actually fined; the fines that were imposed were often at reduced rates; the persecution eased; priests came to recognise that they should not refuse communion to occasional conformists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993264_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993264-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The persecutions did not extinguish the faith, but they tested it sorely. The huge number of Roman Catholics in <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">North</a> in the 1560s disappeared into the general population in part because recusant priests largely served the great Roman Catholic houses, which alone could hide them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993265_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh1993265-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Without the Mass and pastoral care, yeomen, artisans and husbandmen fell into conformism. Roman Catholicism, supported by foreign or expatriate priests, came to be seen as treasonous.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a></div>\n<p>By the time of Elizabeth\'s death a third party had emerged, "perfectly hostile" to Puritans but not adherent to Rome. It preferred the revised <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> of 1559, which was without some of the matters offensive to Roman Catholics.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The recusants had been removed from the centre of the stage. The new dispute was now between the Puritans (who wished to see an end of the prayer book and episcopacy), and this third party (the considerable body of people who looked kindly on the Elizabethan Settlement, who rejected <i><a href="/wiki/Prophesyings" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophesyings">prophesyings</a></i>, whose spirituality had been nourished by the Prayer Book and who preferred the governance of bishops).<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was between these two groups that, after Elizabeth\'s death in 1603, a new, more savage episode of the Reformation was in the process of gestation. During the reigns of the Stuart kings, <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, the battle lines were to become more defined, leading ultimately to the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, the first on English soil to engulf parts of the civilian population. The war was only partly about religion, but the abolition of prayer book and episcopacy by a Puritan Parliament was an element in the causes of the conflict. As historian MacCulloch has noted, the legacy of these tumultuous events can be recognised, throughout the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth</a> (1649\xe2\x80\x9360) and <a href="/wiki/The_Restoration" class="mw-redirect" title="The Restoration">the Restoration</a> that followed it, and beyond. This third party was to become the core of the restored Church of England, but at the price for further division.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=English_Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Historiography of the United Kingdom">Historiography of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The historiography of the English Reformation has seen vigorous clashes among dedicated protagonists and scholars for five centuries.  The main factual details at the national level have been clear since 1900, as laid out for example by <a href="/wiki/James_Anthony_Froude" title="James Anthony Froude">James Anthony Froude</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Pollard" title="Albert Pollard">Albert Pollard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reformation historiography has seen many schools of interpretation with Protestant, Catholic, Anglican historians using their own religious perspectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVidmar2005_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVidmar2005-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In addition there has been a highly influential <a href="/wiki/Whig_history" title="Whig history">Whig</a> interpretation, based on liberal secularized Protestantism, that depicted the Reformation in England, in the words of Ian Hazlitt, as "the midwife delivering England from the Dark Ages to the threshold of modernity, and so a turning point of progress".  Finally among the older schools was a neo-Marxist interpretation that stressed the economic decline of the old elites in the rise of the landed gentry and middle classes. All these approaches still have representatives, but the main thrust of scholarly historiography since the 1970s falls into four groupings or schools, according to Hazlett.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Elton" title="Geoffrey Elton">Geoffrey Elton</a> leads the first faction with an agenda rooted in political historiography. It concentrates on the top of the early modern church-state looking at it at the mechanics of policymaking and the organs of its implementation and enforcement.  The key player for Elton was not Henry VIII, but rather his principal Secretary of State Thomas Cromwell.  Elton downplays the prophetic spirit of the religious reformers in the theology of keen conviction, dismissing them as the meddlesome intrusions from fanatics and bigots.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Secondly, Geoffrey Dickens and others were motivated by a primarily religious perspective. They prioritize the religious and subjective side of the movement.  While recognizing the Reformation was imposed from the top, just as it was everywhere else in Europe, it also responded to aspirations from below.  Dickens has been criticized for underestimating the strength of residual and revived Roman Catholicism, but has been praised for his demonstration of the close ties to European influences. In the Dickens school, <a href="/wiki/David_Loades" title="David Loades">David Loades</a> has stressed the theological importance of the Reformation for Anglo-British development.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Revisionists comprise a third school, led by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Haigh" title="Christopher Haigh">Christopher Haigh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Scarisbrick" title="Jack Scarisbrick">Jack Scarisbrick</a> and numerous other scholars.  Their main achievement was the discovery of an entirely new corpus of primary sources at the local level, leading them to the emphasis on Reformation as it played out on a daily and local basis, with much less emphasis on the control from the top they emphasize turning away from elite sources they emphasize local parish records, diocesan files, guild records, data from boroughs, the courts, and especially telltale individual wills.\n</p><p>Finally, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Collinson" title="Patrick Collinson">Patrick Collinson</a> and others have brought much more precision to the theological landscape, with Calvinist Puritans who were impatient with the Anglican caution sent compromises.  Indeed, the Puritans were a distinct subgroup who did not comprise all of Calvinism.  The Church of England thus emerged as a coalition of factions, all of them Protestant inspiration.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All the recent schools have decentered Henry VIII, and minimized hagiography. They have paid more attention to localities, Catholicism, radicals, and theological niceties.  On Catholicism, the older schools overemphasized <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a> (1470\xe2\x80\x931535), to the neglect of other bishops and factors inside Catholicism.  The older schools too often concentrated on elite London, the newer ones look to the English villages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy2006_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy2006-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span Morris, <i>Europe and England in the Sixteenth century</i>, (Routledge, 1998), p.&#160;172.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFTanner1930" class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Robson_Tanner" title="Joseph Robson Tanner">Tanner, J. R.</a> (1930). <i>Tudor Constitutional Documents A.D. 1485-1603: With an Historical Commentary</i> (2nd ed.). Tanner gives this as "their singular protector, only and supreme lord, and, as far as the law of Christ allows, even Supreme Head".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000118-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrigden2000118_22-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBrigden2000">Brigden 2000</a>, p.&#160;118.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTETanner1930-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanner1930_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTanner1930">Tanner 1930</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">After prolonged debate in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a>, it was clear they would not reach unanimity over the Bill\xe2\x80\x94so Henry ordered a division. He commanded those in favour of his success and the "welfare of the realm" to one side of the House, and those who opposed him and the Bill to the other. Thus, he obtained a majority.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFElton1982" class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Elton" title="Geoffrey Elton">Elton, G.
